    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8590399381890046206">2009 PEN Translation Prize Finalist<br/><br/> The logic of capitalism, boom and bust, is unremitting and unforgiving. But what happens to human feeling in a completely commodified world? In The Post-Office Girl, Stefan Zweig, a deep analyst of the human passions, lays bare the private life of capitalism.<br/><br/>Christine toils in a provincial post office in post–World War I Austria, a count</span>
  <span id="freeText8590399381890046206" style="display:none">2009 PEN Translation Prize Finalist<br /><br /> The logic of capitalism, boom and bust, is unremitting and unforgiving. But what happens to human feeling in a completely commodified world? In The Post-Office Girl, Stefan Zweig, a deep analyst of the human passions, lays bare the private life of capitalism.<br /><br />Christine toils in a provincial post office in post–World War I Austria, a country gripped by unemployment. Out of the blue, a telegram arrives from Christine’s rich American aunt inviting her to a resort in the Swiss Alps. Christine is immediately swept up into a world of inconceivable wealth and unleashed desire. She feels herself utterly transformed: nothing is impossible. But then, abruptly, her aunt cuts her loose. Christine returns to the post office, where yes, nothing will ever be the same again.<br /><br />Christine meets Ferdinand, a bitter war veteran and disappointed architect, who works construction jobs when he can get them. They are drawn to each other, even as they are crushed by a sense of deprivation, of anger and shame. Work, politics, love, sex: everything is impossible for them. Life is meaningless, unless, through one desperate and decisive act, they can secretly remake their world from within.<br /><br />Cinderella meets Bonnie and Clyde in Zweig’s haunting and hard-as-nails novel, completed during the 1930s, as he was driven by the Nazis into exile, but left unpublished at the time of his death. The Post-Office Girl, available here for the first time in English, transforms our image of a modern master’s achievement.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8590399381890046206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/73995166-andrew-cooper"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1511122029i/73995166._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/73995166-andrew-cooper">Andrew Cooper</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I agree with your disconnect issue throughout Part 2. Not take away from the two other responses, but I felt Part 1 was nicely tidied up at the break,<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I agree with your disconnect issue throughout Part 2. Not take away from the two other responses, but I felt Part 1 was nicely tidied up at the break, whereas Part 2 was jumbled at the end. The book basically ends 1.5 pages after the theft plan+summary which was published in its entirety. That alone was too disruptive that close to the end of action.<br /><br />Actually, I would have enjoyed the book if it ended directly after Part 1 as a tragedy. It felt so emotional and real at that point, whereas Zweig lost his captive narration in Part 2 once he brought in Ferdinand's capitalism vs revolutionary vs complacency argument in the Vienna livingroom. Although it could be said, the novella was written to demonstrate this argument and that's why it ended as it did.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2824064137" />

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2824064137?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=german-authors">german-authors</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2824064137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15896972161504984443">A story of poverty, despair and disillusioned lives. (Another ‘light’ read, LOL.) A young woman, a post office clerk in Austria, has lived in poverty supporting her mother most of her life. She was born in 1898 and thus in her teens went through the deprivations of WW I. “The war stole her decade of youth.” Her small village offers no marriage prospects. <br/><br/>Suddenly a wealthy aunt and uncle from the United States invite her to visit with them on vacation in the Swiss Alps. For a little more than on</span>
  <span id="freeText15896972161504984443" style="display:none">A story of poverty, despair and disillusioned lives. (Another ‘light’ read, LOL.) A young woman, a post office clerk in Austria, has lived in poverty supporting her mother most of her life. She was born in 1898 and thus in her teens went through the deprivations of WW I. “The war stole her decade of youth.” Her small village offers no marriage prospects. <br><br>Suddenly a wealthy aunt and uncle from the United States invite her to visit with them on vacation in the Swiss Alps. For a little more than one week, the young woman enters a whirlpool of an unimaginable life. Her aunt buys her clothes and cosmetics and re-styles her hair – not realizing she spent an amount equal to her niece’s annual salary. Hotels, dancing, gambling, restaurants, room service and maids, fast cars (and petting with the wealthy men in them). Her uncle shares a single gambling bet with her, not realizing he has given her another half of her annual salary. It’s TOO much. I won’t give a spoiler here but it all ends abruptly and she goes back to her village and her job. Her mother soon dies.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1558091336i/27529159._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>She plunges into despair at her again-empty life. She turns into a petty tyrant at the post office, snapping at customers and making them strictly follow all the stupid bureaucratic rules. She has a sister in Vienna, an hour’s train ride away, and here she finally meets her soulmate - an old army buddy of her brother-in-law. The man she meets is equally poverty-stricken and disillusioned with life because he has crippled hands from the war but no disability pension. Jobs where you don’t need to use your hands are hard to come by as the depression is looming. Then he loses that job and he’s worried about turning into a beggar on the streets. <br><br>They both come to believe in the “horrible purposelessness of life:” “Every morning when I go to work I see people coming out of their front doors, underslept, cheerless, their faces blank, see them going to work that they haven’t chosen and have no love for and that means nothing to them, and then again in the evening I see them in the streetcars on their way back, their expressions leaden, their feet leaden, all of them exhausted for no good reason, or some reason they don’t understand.” <br><br>They meet weekly in Vienna but have no money to really enjoy themselves. They can’t even afford a hotel to be intimate – they tried a flophouse and she is terrified to go back after it was raided by the police looking for prostitutes. They are so disillusioned that he talks her into committing suicide with him. <br>(He has a pistol from the war.)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1558091336i/27529160._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This incident about joint suicide eerily foreshadows the way the author will die. He and his second wife killed themselves with poison in exile in Brazil in 1942. Zweig fled Austria as he was Jewish. <br><br>But once you’re open to suicide, other unthinkable possibilities occur to you. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The man comes to realize from conversations with the woman that at the end of the month, her post office has enough cash on hand to last them for two or three years. He writes out an elaborately detailed written plan about how she will steal the money, cover their tracks, and they will escape to Paris and maybe later move to America. She agrees. Will it happen? Will it all work out?  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1558091336i/27529161._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>There's good writing: <br><br>“Someone who’s on top of the world isn’t much of an observer: happy people are poor psychologists. But someone who’s troubled about something is on the alert. The perceived threat sharpens his senses – he takes in more than he usually does.”<br><br>“ ‘No, no,’ Christine says, or rather her lips say it, the way a patient going under anesthesia might continue counting after losing consciousness.” <br><br>Zweig (1881-1942) was a prolific author with 25 or so books. Only a few, such as Post Office Girl, are full novels – many are novellas and collections of short stories. He was at one time considered the most translated author in the world.<br><br>Top photo: Park Hotel in Vitznau, Switzerland from edge.media.datahc.com<br><br>Middle photo: Vienna in the early 1900’s from cliomuse.com <br><br>Photo of the author and his second wife, Lotte from Flickr, Creative Commons<br><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="15896972161504984443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_712901838" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/712901838" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/712901838?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783596258741" href="/book/show/1210461.Rausch_der_Verwandlung_Roman_aus_dem_Nachla_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=austro-hungary">austro-hungary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-german">fiction-german</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer712901838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7482457591409174836"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1400258064i/9656341._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><br/><b>FRAGMENTED IDENTITIES / FRAGMENTED NOVEL</b><br/><br/>One has to approach this novella with trepidation. Zweig did not publish it. The first and posthumous edition is from 1982, after a considerable reworking of Zweig’s drafts by <b>Knut Beck</b>. Zweig took his own life in a planned manner in February 1942, but before doing so he had sent to his publisher two manuscripts which he had just finished: his memoirs or <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1508972.Die_Welt_von_Gestern__Erinnerungen_eines_Europ_ers' title='Die Welt von Gestern. Erinnerungen eines Europäers by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Die Welt von Gestern. Erinnerungen eines Europäers</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1016722.Schachnovelle' title='Schachnovelle by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Schachnovelle</a>. To leave this earlier work unfini</span>
  <span id="freeText7482457591409174836" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1400258064i/9656341._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br><b>FRAGMENTED IDENTITIES / FRAGMENTED NOVEL</b><br><br>One has to approach this novella with trepidation. Zweig did not publish it. The first and posthumous edition is from 1982, after a considerable reworking of Zweig’s drafts by <b>Knut Beck</b>. Zweig took his own life in a planned manner in February 1942, but before doing so he had sent to his publisher two manuscripts which he had just finished: his memoirs or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1508972.Die_Welt_von_Gestern__Erinnerungen_eines_Europ_ers" title="Die Welt von Gestern. Erinnerungen eines Europäers by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Die Welt von Gestern. Erinnerungen eines Europäers</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1016722.Schachnovelle" title="Schachnovelle by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Schachnovelle</a>. To leave this earlier work unfinished was his decision.<br><br>The German edition has a most interesting <b>Afterword</b> by Beck, the paramount editor, in which he explains the history of the composition. Beck quotes from a letter by Zweig from 1931 in which he confides that he is stuck in the middle of the novel and that he felt as in front of a ditch (<i>ein tiefer Graben</i>). He probably stopped there and years later, when he was already living in London, took it up again. This was going to be his first novel.<br><br>We don’t even have his title for the work. It has been published in German under <i>Der Rausch der Verwandlung</i> (The Intoxication of Transformation), and this title has been kept literal in the translations into French and Spanish. It originates in a couple of fragments from the book and Beck picked it. For the English edition, or <b>The Post Office Girl</b>, my understanding is that the very different title was borrowed from a tag used by Donald Prater <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59154.European_Of_Yesterday_A_Biography_Of_Stefan_Zweig" title="European Of Yesterday A Biography Of Stefan Zweig by Donald A. Prater" rel="nofollow noopener">European Of Yesterday: A Biography Of Stefan Zweig</a> in his biography from the 1970s (mentioned by Beck as <i>Postfräuleingeschichte</i>). <br><br>I have to say that I don’t like this English title. It is too prosaic and there is an enchanting amount of lyricism in this novel. But the German title does not entirely suit it either, or may be it suits half of the work. <br><br>But that is just it: this title suits the part that Zweig wrote before he felt to be in front of a hole.<br><br>Because the novel itself, in its current form, is somewhat fragmented, as was its conception. And this I clearly felt while I was reading it. Disconcerted, I posted an update stating that I did not know where Zweig was going.<br><br>In the first part, and the English edition has indeed adroitly created two separate parts, Zweig presents in his very characteristic way what has been called an existential version of the <b>Cinderella</b> story. But Zweig is our literary cardiologist. He is the author who with his pen can listen to a heart pulsating and give us, flowingly, the life that emanates from that inner pump. Using expansive imagery in which he equates the landscape in the Alps with the inner workings of a young woman’s heart he brings forth issues of identity – and fragmentation. Because in taking her pulse he has detected more than one heart.<br><br>And so Zweig shows us that traveling can exert its transforming power. It can tear away from the individual the protecting shell of conventionality. The metamorphosis thus began and <b>Christine Hoflehner</b> becomes the much happier <b>Christiane van Boolen</b>. And questions are raised. What constitutes one’s identity? How come a different self can be conjured up by a change of setting and a change of clothes? Is materiality so determinant for the soul then? Do we have more than one “I” if one starts accumulating, deep in one’s consciousness, different experiences from those lived in the past? How many "selves" do we have?<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1400258718i/9656423.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>Zweig dwelt on this idea, and on his memories from his earlier time in Engadin, when he first worked on this novel. When he returned to the work, his own life and his own circumstances had changed. He was living in exile and in distraught awareness of the dreadful political situations with as yet unforeseen consequences that were developing in his and other countries. He was also going through the divorce from his first wife and the marriage to his young secretary.<br><br>Zweig himself was grappling with his newly transforming identity as he undertook the continuation of his story and the search for the soul of Christine. In a different tone he proceeded to project a greater emphasis on the limitations that prevent full control of one’s life and destiny. With this new focal point social and political circumstances, and money, take on an unexpected role. Money, the magic substance that can become the mirror of one’s desires, becomes the helm that could allow a person to steer towards the desired self.<br><br>Zweig however felt that he had not succeeded in this literary attempt and left the drafts on loose sheets. And Beck’s archivist work could only leave us a novella that did not quite become a novel. Its modernist tint and ending may not be intentional. <br><br>With interest I learn, also from Beck, that in the early 1940s Zweig took his manuscript up again, but this time to adapt it to a film. He worked very closely with <b>Berthold Viertel</b> on the new script. It was filmed, also posthumously, in 1950 as <i>Das Verstohlene Jahr</i>. The basic story had been transformed to include an additional episode (the sabbatical year of a Composer) not present in the book as Beck has graciously delivered it to us.<br><br><br>Now I am on the search for that film.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="7482457591409174836" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_702025611" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/702025611" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1938805-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442608867p2/1938805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/702025611?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/1938805-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer702025611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6552637227720412869">The world would be a better place if we could all just agree to read more Stefan Zweig. Is that so hard?<br/><br/>I have a pet theory, my own personal belief why Stefan has been neglected. Before the dawn of the e-book, Zweig's novels were shelved in libraries and bookstores in that alphabetical no-man's land: the tail end of the last shelf, right next to those spare metal bookends that look like jetsam from the Millenium Falcon. I can honestly say I've never once, ever, browsed without purpose in the Z's</span>
  <span id="freeText6552637227720412869" style="display:none">The world would be a better place if we could all just agree to read more Stefan Zweig. Is that so hard?<br /><br />I have a pet theory, my own personal belief why Stefan has been neglected. Before the dawn of the e-book, Zweig's novels were shelved in libraries and bookstores in that alphabetical no-man's land: the tail end of the last shelf, right next to those spare metal bookends that look like jetsam from the Millenium Falcon. I can honestly say I've never once, ever, browsed without purpose in the Z's for a book. Zw? You're just asking to be the coaster for some asshat's unfinished Starbucks.<br /><br />This is only the second Zweig I've read, both ebooks (I will remedy this!) and I am going to, 100%, read everything he has written. Several of you Goodread chums have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story" title="Chess Story by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Chess Story</a> and know how Zweig can write beautiful prose and tell a great story. His characters are rich, his settings vibrant and the pacing of his books is just ... enviable. <br /><br />This novel is a gem and centers around the maxim that if money can't buy you happiness, it certainly can pay-off a lot of the blues. I'm not a plot recapper - you can get a good idea of the story from the GR synopsis. But this book is so much more than what happens. Here's a li'l something from Stefan:<br /><br /><i>"The vast power of money, mighty when you have it and mightier when you don't, with its divine gift of freedom and the demonic fury it unleashes on those forced to do without it..."</i><br /><br />This story also has one of best endings I've read in a long while. I love pomo, experimental and all types of fiction. But sometimes I just want a really great story. And this book is exactly that.<br /><br />Thanks much to my two GR friends, Proustitue and Kris for insisting I read Zweig.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6552637227720412869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_941436556" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/941436556" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5304930-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603654425p2/5304930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/941436556?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/5304930-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5304930-mary?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5304930-mary?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5304930-mary?shelf=2016">2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5304930-mary?shelf=austria">austria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer941436556" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11079416316089064047">With some things in life, you can’t go back once you’ve crossed a line. For me it was travel. Once my teenage self left my home country for the first time I was hooked, and I never looked at anything in the same way again. To go back to a life without traveling was unthinkable. With the post-office girl, it’s a brief foray into the world of the elite, a glimpse into what life is like on the other side. When circumstances force you back to your prosaic life, the bitterness that seeps into your bl</span>
  <span id="freeText11079416316089064047" style="display:none">With some things in life, you can’t go back once you’ve crossed a line. For me it was travel. Once my teenage self left my home country for the first time I was hooked, and I never looked at anything in the same way again. To go back to a life without traveling was unthinkable. With the post-office girl, it’s a brief foray into the world of the elite, a glimpse into what life is like on the other side. When circumstances force you back to your prosaic life, the bitterness that seeps into your blood is poison and you wonder if maybe you were better off staying ignorant. The post-office girl can’t cope and only when a seemingly likeminded disgruntled soul crosses her path do we think that maybe there might be some kind of redemption. <br /><br />The two parts of this novel seem a little disconnected and almost feel like two partial ideas for two different novels. I didn’t care. I loved it. And then it all ends on a cliffhanger, which should be expected for an unfinished work, and I’m not sure it’s entirely unsatisfying because I finished this about 12 hours ago and am still thinking about it. Do they do it, or don’t they? Does it matter? Maybe we take both forks in the road. Maybe we take neither. Maybe all these plans were in our head all along and we’re still toiling away behind our desk at the job we hate in the town we hate and it was all a fantasy. <br /><br />Zweig has yet to steer me wrong and this might be my favorite of his works so far. So deeply, deeply sad.<br /><br /><i>And suddenly you’re old and faded and you die and you don’t know anything and you never lived and you never knew anything.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11079416316089064047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_977784930" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/977784930" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/977784930?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cheryl by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2034949-nidhi-singh">Nidhi Singh</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fav-authors">fav-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=mesmerizing">mesmerizing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=war-stories">war-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer977784930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15304449585198503270"><i>She feels borne along, carried by the wind. She was a child the last time she flew like this. This is the beginning of the delirium of transformation.</i><br/><br/>To live again, after experiencing the brutality of war. To lose one's parent, one's home, one's trajectory; to feel mentally crippled after war has stripped one of everything one thought she was or could become, everything one thought about life. To see life anew, after being given a chance. To hope. To dream. To try and be 'normal' again: never af</span>
  <span id="freeText15304449585198503270" style="display:none"><i>She feels borne along, carried by the wind. She was a child the last time she flew like this. This is the beginning of the delirium of transformation.</i><br><br>To live again, after experiencing the brutality of war. To lose one's parent, one's home, one's trajectory; to feel mentally crippled after war has stripped one of everything one thought she was or could become, everything one thought about life. To see life anew, after being given a chance. To hope. To dream. To try and be 'normal' again: never afraid of humans and their motives, never afraid that somehow death's grip will find you ("survivor's guilt"), never afraid of the nightmares that follow sleep. To feel like an ordinary person with ordinary dreams that are possible. To feel like one is accepted at the table spread for many, that one can achieve with hard work and determination. And then to watch it all fade away as humans show you once more, how despicable humanity can be. <br><br>Christine, <i>The Post-Office Girl,</i> works in solitude each day at a routine job that brings her gloom, even though she knows that for a woman living in a village where there are few jobs available after the war, hers is a coveted position. One day she is given a second chance at life when her rich aunt calls her to Pontresina for vacation. She is stunned by the lavish hotels and rich men and women in their twenties who think she carries her aunt's well-received last name. She is treated like royalty, her wardrobe and dinner conversation transformed. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1488652313i/22140884._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>She thinks she is this transformed individual of luxury, until her new friends learn she's really from the village. Her life is again torn to shreds and for a war survivor, this can be disconcerting. She meets another survivor, a war veteran. He too had dreams, he too was intelligent, an aspiring architect, but those years of war took pieces away from his life and when he returns, an injured veteran, he finds that others have his coveted position and that no one is willing to give him a chance. These two hopeless beings become tangled in thought, for even without love, they share trust.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1488652313i/22140883._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>
  <blockquote>Why breathe this in day after day, knowing that there's another world out there somewhere, the real one, and in herself another person, who is suffocating, being poisoned, in this miasma…suddenly she hates everyone and everything, herself and everyone else, wealth and property, everything about this hard, unendurable, incomprehensible life.</blockquote>
</b><br><br>Zweig is ruthless in his reveal of transformation and as usual, his psychological depictions are stunning because of his way with words. He forces the reader to examine the undertones of war. He knows how to turn introspection into imagery, bringing a reader so close to a character's inner thoughts that one feels the words breathe. I may not understand Christine's decision at the end, but I certainly understand her. Those tortured feelings from war are ones one never can seem to get rid of, and no one except one who has experienced them, can understand. Zweig wrote this when he was forced into exile by the Nazis, and one can sense his despair on each page. Christine's meandering thought and the wander from first-person to third-person perspective may be at times jolting and confusing for the restless reader, but a closer read reveals a method to the madness. I was afraid to read this novel after reading Zweig's novellas; since he is the king of the fictional short form, I wondered if he would conquer this long form (I excluded his nonfiction as I pondered this). But I'm now learning that for me, reading Zweig is like reading Baldwin - those two just seem to rub me in all the right places.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15304449585198503270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Some books grip me, make me react in a negative way, putting me in a psychological state of melancholia and grief, but it's rare that I feel as if I'm inside the character's head as he/she is speaking to me.         <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780954221720" href="/book/show/6131995-the-post-office-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2034949-nidhi-singh?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2034949-nidhi-singh?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2034949-nidhi-singh?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer932125350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10169921129624907279"><i>
  <blockquote>When will it be me? When will it be my turn? What have I been dreaming about during these long empty mornings if not about being free someday from this meaningless grind, this deadly race against time? Relaxing for once, having some unbroken time to myself, not always in shreds, in shards so tiny you could cut your finger on them.</blockquote>
</i><br/><br/>Life can sometimes seem to be arrested in a state of perpetual halt; the waiting for your chance that never ever comes. Not a moment of respite, not a moment withou</span>
  <span id="freeText10169921129624907279" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>When will it be me? When will it be my turn? What have I been dreaming about during these long empty mornings if not about being free someday from this meaningless grind, this deadly race against time? Relaxing for once, having some unbroken time to myself, not always in shreds, in shards so tiny you could cut your finger on them.</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />Life can sometimes seem to be arrested in a state of perpetual halt; the waiting for your chance that never ever comes. Not a moment of respite, not a moment without worry. Not a moment that isn’t barely scraped from the blatant drudgery of routine. No rest, no sleep that doesn’t fear waking up. Christine’s life has no joy, no enthusiasm, no happiness. The days are of boredom and monotony, packed up into the office-routine, like used giveaway clothes in a cardboard box. It is the feeling of not having a personal moment of living dedicated just to oneself, to solely being happy. And of being caged forever in this drabness, like being stuck in a compartment with rapidly depleting air. A joyless living, a resignation, still with something sleeping inside that could be scratched back to consciousness.<br /> <br /><i>
  <blockquote>Meat too expensive, butter too expensive, a pair of shoes, too expensive: Christine hardly dares to breathe for fear it might be too expensive.</blockquote>
</i><br /> <br />War has ruined childhoods, it has destroyed families, it has afflicted people with a nightmarish living. War has killed desire, it has bred ennui and apathy, and worst of all it has bred unrelenting poverty. Zweig frequently describes the characters in this book in terms of their stark possessions. A tattered coat, its threadbare elbows, a cheap dirty shirt, a flat straw suitcase, a ragged umbrella, and one could conjure up a person altogether, could tell their whole stories. Christine’s mother’s life could be recounted with the meager possessions she left behind. Such images graze upon the mind; the bareness of existence, its insufficiency. <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>How could she ever wear such splendid and fragile treasures without constantly worrying? How do you walk, how do you move in such mist of color and light? Don’t you have to learn how to wear clothes like these?</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />It is interesting to mark the Cinderella-like transformations in Christine. Pretty clothes, new shoes; they highlight her sketchy outline and fill it with colors of visibility. It is as if she is a tangible person for the first time. Then lifeless and now intoxicated with life, with a new feeling of joy blazing inside. <i>This rare swoon of lying back and being ministered to, this strange voluptuous feeling you haven’t experienced in years, in decades</i>. The sensuality of this new feeling has been depicted as vividly as taking the first bite of a ripe fruit, as gulping in the freshness of morning air, and running down the path of one’s own self-discovery. It is the joy of being noticed, appreciated, desired. For the first time. The awareness of one’s own youth, attractiveness, desirability and desires. That such a phase is so short-lived, yet extremely potent in its possibilities makes Christine’s downfall extremely painful. The anti-climax seems like a sudden and rude cessation. <br /><br /><i>
  <blockquote>But how can I hide, how can I disappear quickly before anyone sees me and takes offense.</blockquote>
</i><br /><br />There is always the fear of betraying her poverty, her middle-class bearing to the contemptuous gaze of the rich. With such poverty, how hard it is not to be unsure of oneself. For Zweig’s protagonists, poverty is a great assault to pride because it perpetually denies them their rightful places in the world. It burrows deep holes in their withered lives making them dysfunctional individuals, impervious to genuine love, incapable to love fully. It is amazing how Zweig portrays these fully rounded characters with their continued apathy, their shrunken capacity to love, their lessened ability to live. There is a lack of stability, of dignity, of a personal space. It is an existence that is fractured, sullied and threatened. It keeps running around the same unbreakable circle of regularity and powerlessness. There are some who are hyper aware, some who are doused in sinuous complacence. Some have devised their own strategies of living their reduced lives, some refuse not to see what has been obfuscated, not to want what has been denied. They can’t be placidly engulfed in an existence that is scanty, at most a concession.<br /> <br /><i>
  <blockquote>How terrible it is to have to live here, and why, who’s it for? Why breathe this in day after day, knowing that there’s another world out there somewhere, the real one, and in herself another person, who is suffocating, being poisoned, in this miasma. Her nerves are jangling. She throws herself down onto the bed fully clothed, biting down hard on the pillow to keep from screaming with helpless hatred. Because suddenly she hates everyone and everything, herself and everyone else, wealth and poverty, everything about this hard, unendurable, incomprehensible life.</blockquote>
</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10169921129624907279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_95633585" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95633585" />
    <a title="Anastasia Fitzgerald-Beaumont" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3044221-anastasia-fitzgerald-beaumont"><img alt="Anastasia Fitzgerald-Beaumont" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361149670p2/3044221.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/95633585?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anastasia Fitzgerald-Beaumont" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anastasia Fitzgerald-Beaumont" href="/user/show/3044221-anastasia-fitzgerald-beaumont">Anastasia Fitzgerald-Beaumont</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95633585" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14003881768498105867">This is a novel for today, an odd thing to say, considering it was written almost seventy years ago. It's a tragic version of the Cinderella story, a version with no glass slipper and no Prince Charming; it's a story of a girl taken to the heights only to be plunged back into the depths.<br/><br/>The author, Stephan Zweig, though not that well known in the English-speaking world, is probably the best late representative of the culture of old Vienna, that urbane, tolerant, sophisticated and brilliant world</span>
  <span id="freeText14003881768498105867" style="display:none">This is a novel for today, an odd thing to say, considering it was written almost seventy years ago. It's a tragic version of the Cinderella story, a version with no glass slipper and no Prince Charming; it's a story of a girl taken to the heights only to be plunged back into the depths.<br /><br />The author, Stephan Zweig, though not that well known in the English-speaking world, is probably the best late representative of the culture of old Vienna, that urbane, tolerant, sophisticated and brilliant world, swept away forever by the rise of the Nazis. <br /><br />His oeuvre covered such a wide area of intellectual life: he was a biographer, playwright, journalist, short story writer and novelist. After Hitler came to power Zweig left his native Austria, taking refuge in England, America and finally in Brazil, where he and his second wife committed suicide in 1942 in a mood of despair over a possible German victory in the War. The manuscript of his second novel was found among his papers. Remarkably it was to be forty years after his death it was published in Germany for the first time, under the title Rausch der Verwandlung-The Intoxication of Transformation. In 2008 it was translated and published in English as The Post Office Girl. <br /><br />Written in a simple, fast-paced and intoxication style, it tells the story of Christine Hoflehner, a woman in her late twenties who manages a small provincial post office in Austria, a country only just emerging from the trauma of the First World War and the economic and social dislocation that followed. <br /><br />The action begins in 1926, when Christine is twenty-eight years old and living with her elderly mother, whose health has been ruined by her past experiences. The Hoflehners, once a prosperous and middle-class family, have, like so many others of the time, been brought close to ruin by the war and its after-effects. Christine, a poorly paid civil servant, recognises that life is passing her by; that her horizons are always likely to be confining and confined. Even so, there is a kind of resigned acceptance in this destiny. But then a telegram arrives from Clara, her rich American aunt, holidaying with her husband in Switzerland. <br /><br />As if a fairy-godmother had appeared, Christine is lifted out of the tedium and poverty into a brilliant world, a world full of rich and glamorous people. Dowdy and badly dressed when she arrived at the luxury Swiss hotel where her relatives are staying, she is transformed in dress and appearance by her aunt. Hesitant at first, Christine is drawn into the delights of her surroundings. All at once everything is possible. Losing all inhibition, Christine enjoys the company of new friends, of men who find her beautiful and beguiling, of people whose life and experiences have been so different to her own. She learns to forget. But then the dream ends, abruptly and cruelly. It's midnight; the clock is striking. Discarded by her aunt, she is thrown back into her old world.<br /><br />It's at this point that the full tragedy of Christine's story is realised. What was tolerable before is now intolerable. Before there was nothing that stood in contrast to the tedium of her daily life; now there is. A gate was opened briefly, only to close forever. New forms of bitterness and despair set in only relieved, to a degree, when she meets Ferdinand, even bitterer than Christine. What follows is a love affair of a kind, limping and unsatisfactory, of two people bound by a mutual sense of rejection. <br /><br />This is a fairy tale with no happy ending. In fact it might be said to have no ending at all. Remember it's an unfinished book, and the last few pages read almost as if the author is outlining possible future developments. To that degree the conclusion, such as it is, might even said to be abrupt. But there again, this might conceivably have been what Zweig wanted. After all, life is abrupt. No matter; it's one of those books that make a lasting impression, one that will stay with me for a long time to come.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14003881768498105867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2347057414" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2347057414" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2347057414?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=austrian">austrian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=nyrb-classics">nyrb-classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2347057414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9014491870957372290">It would be wrong to criticize the author, who held this back from publication. It's disjointed and incomplete, as Zweig surely knew. It deserves a fragmented review. And I'm the obliging type.<br/><br/>First the bromides. Or axioms, or samplers. Those things that you read and nod to, but on second glance, well, here:<br/><br/><b>--</b> <i>Happy people are poor psychologists.</i><br/><br/><b>--</b> <i>Malice is always lucky.</i><br/><br/><b>--</b> <i>The subject of a rumor is always the last to hear it.</i><br/><br/><b>--</b> <i>It's one of the few advantages of age that one is rarely wrong abou</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9014491870957372290" style="display:none">It would be wrong to criticize the author, who held this back from publication. It's disjointed and incomplete, as Zweig surely knew. It deserves a fragmented review. And I'm the obliging type.<br /><br />First the bromides. Or axioms, or samplers. Those things that you read and nod to, but on second glance, well, here:<br /><br /><b>--</b> <i>Happy people are poor psychologists.</i><br /><br /><b>--</b> <i>Malice is always lucky.</i><br /><br /><b>--</b> <i>The subject of a rumor is always the last to hear it.</i><br /><br /><b>--</b> <i>It's one of the few advantages of age that one is rarely wrong about people.</i><br /><br />One of these is true. One of these is false. And two are just silly. I found them in a mere seven pages, when the author must have been having a particularly aphoristic afternoon.<br /><br />It may be true that there is a small number of people I might warm to, but whiny nihilists will not be among them.<br /><br />When all is hopeless - meaning your job sucks and true love eludes - there are, apparently, only two choices: suicide for two, or rob a bank.<br /><br />Kind of chilling that Zweig was already thinking about option 1, a good decade before he, you know, chose option 1.<br /><br />Zweig wrote a page-turner in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59149.Beware_of_Pity" title="Beware of Pity by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener"> Beware of Pity</a>, and he nailed what he was always trying to say in the brief <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story" title="Chess Story by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener"> Chess Story</a>. But he didn't want you to read this one.</span>

<div id="review_734750676" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/734750676" />
    <a title="Orsolya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5647191-orsolya"><img alt="Orsolya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603139114p2/5647191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/734750676?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orsolya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orsolya" href="/user/show/5647191-orsolya">Orsolya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=library-2">library-2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer734750676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer248006558653069627">The themes present in life during the grips of post-WWI stricken Austria (poverty, death, sickness, class distinctions); are sadly harsh realties that are also relatable in the modern day. Stefan Zweig explores the story of Christine, a poor 28-year-old Austrian woman who briefly enjoys the lap of luxury with her Aunt on a vacation but then is sent back to her lower-class private hell in, “The Post-Office Girl”. <br/><br/>Zweig’s “The Post Office Girl” is nothing short of a literary classic—a masterpiece </span>
  <span id="freeText248006558653069627" style="display:none">The themes present in life during the grips of post-WWI stricken Austria (poverty, death, sickness, class distinctions); are sadly harsh realties that are also relatable in the modern day. Stefan Zweig explores the story of Christine, a poor 28-year-old Austrian woman who briefly enjoys the lap of luxury with her Aunt on a vacation but then is sent back to her lower-class private hell in, “The Post-Office Girl”. <br /><br />Zweig’s “The Post Office Girl” is nothing short of a literary classic—a masterpiece in its own right. Zweig dives right into the plot in the strain of a novella or short story without a “proper” buildup or introduction to the characters and yet, all is revealed on such an intimate and detailed level that the reader truly experiences all events and the psyches of Christine and the other characters. This is accented by delightful prose; which even with little dialogue transports the reader with vivid imagery and strong descriptions: the novel feels almost like watching a film. <br /><br />One of the finest highlights of “The Post Office Girl” is Zweig’s amazing ability to demonstrate the emotions and actions of all the characters on a psychological level despite their ages, race, or gender. Zweig must have been an empath as few truly understand people on such an altitude. This makes not only the story in “The Post Office Girl” stand out but also offers the reader a sort of comfort and “food for thought”. <br /><br />“The Post Office Girl” explores various themes of philosophical merit which brings to light the struggles of post- WWI Europe but also correlates these to any era and place which elevates the novel from simply being a dated HF story and enters the realm of a classical novel. <br /><br />There are moments in the “The Post Office Girl” in terms of metaphors/ symbolism and emotional exchanges that are so raw and strong; that one can’t help but be taken aback (in the best way possible). Sometimes, “The Post Office Girl” requires a break from reading in order to take it all in (<u>Note:</u> “The Post Office Girl” is divided into two parts but there are no chapter breaks). <br /><br />Zweig excels at presenting a story which is on the brink of improvisation: nothing is foreshadowed and much is unexpected which compels the story. This is also driven by the almost frantic pace of moments of stream of consciousness; adding layers to “The Post Office Girl”. <br /><br />The climax of “The Post Office Girl” is unexpected, complex, and moving. Unfortunately, the story then takes an abrupt turn versus riding the wave and concludes the novel abruptly with unanswered questions and frankly: in a boring way. This weakens the strength of the entire book, unfortunately. <br /><br />Even with the poor ending; “The Post Office Girl” is an amazing piece of writing which accurately portrays poverty, depression, and struggles in a psychological way. “The Post Office Girl” is recommended for those who enjoy character/life studies or those who have similar paths in their lives as the text is refreshingly relatable and wonderfully written. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="248006558653069627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1652605981" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1652605981" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1652605981?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=poverty">poverty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=austrian-lit">austrian-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1652605981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4706102716103727804">I wanted to read something by Stefan Zweig because his writing was apparently the inspiration for ‘The Grand Budapest Hotel’, one my favourite films of 2014. I picked ‘The Post Office Girl’ because a brief plot description proved intriguing: an impoverished young woman works at a post office. She is whisked away from her life of drudgery for a holiday by a rich relative, then has to go back to her old life. A simple plot, but one rife with dramatic possibility. From the Wes Anderson film, I anti</span>
  <span id="freeText4706102716103727804" style="display:none">I wanted to read something by Stefan Zweig because his writing was apparently the inspiration for ‘The Grand Budapest Hotel’, one my favourite films of 2014. I picked ‘The Post Office Girl’ because a brief plot description proved intriguing: an impoverished young woman works at a post office. She is whisked away from her life of drudgery for a holiday by a rich relative, then has to go back to her old life. A simple plot, but one rife with dramatic possibility. From the Wes Anderson film, I anticipated something droll, mannered, yet with some sharp edges. How I underestimated Zweig! ‘The Post Office Girl’ has an incredible intensity of feeling. When Christine is torn away from her idyllic holiday, it’s nearly physically painful to read. When she meets someone else of equal bitterness, the feeling of recognition is like a static shock. And when one plan succeeds another towards the end, the wild hope is enough to make you gasp and clench your fists.<br /><br />Rather than the film, I was reminded of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1220032.The_Rector_s_Daughter" title="The Rector's Daughter by F.M. Mayor" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rector's Daughter</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/319238.A_Clergyman_s_Daughter" title="A Clergyman's Daughter by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clergyman's Daughter</a>, both of which follow women with limited, lonely lives. Although they are good novels, in fact the former is brilliant, I like ‘The Post Office Girl’ more than either. As well as conveying beautifully the emotions of the titular Christine, Zweig’s novel makes much wider social points with astonishing power. Christine’s life in 1920s Austria has been blighted by the First World War and its aftermath. More generally, she only realises how wretched her existence is once she’s had a short insight into the world of wealth and privilege. This novel is in fact a vivid, beautiful, scathing indictment of inequality such as I’ve very rarely encountered in fiction. Part of Zweig’s genius is to fully explore the interdependence of the practical and emotional toll that poverty takes on Christine – both her shabby clothes and the depth of her feelings about them. Although the secondary characters are neatly drawn, indeed fascinating in the case of the more prominent ones, this is Christine’s novel. It is truly brilliant that Zweig has created such a sympathetic, nuanced, unusual character whilst also using her as a very pointed socio-political commentary. She is a person first and foremost, yet the symbolism also works well. The only other writer I can think of who walks that line so deftly is Zola in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28407.Germinal__Les_Rougon_Macquart___13_" title="Germinal (Les Rougon-Macquart, #13) by Émile Zola" rel="nofollow noopener">Germinal</a>. Orwell doesn’t manage it in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/319238.A_Clergyman_s_Daughter" title="A Clergyman's Daughter by George Orwell" rel="nofollow noopener">A Clergyman's Daughter</a>; his main character is too much symbol and not enough person. Whereas in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1220032.The_Rector_s_Daughter" title="The Rector's Daughter by F.M. Mayor" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rector's Daughter</a> there is no real attempt at wider social commentary and the individual predominates.<br /><br />In short, Zweig really blew me away. It’s as if I was expecting a frothy decaf cappuccino and found myself drinking a triple expresso. I will definitely be searching the library for more of his work. More amazing still, this is not a novel that he considered ready for publication! I’m very glad that his posthumous wishes were not respected in this case, as what a loss that would have been. ‘The Post Office Girl’ does end in a distinctly abrupt manner, but in a very narratively satisfying place. The reader is then free to contemplate what most plausibly happened next, what you would really like to happen next, and the gap between the two. I must also add that the translation is wonderful and Zweig has a magnificent turn of phrase. Really, I should stop throwing around the hyperbolic adjectives and just heartily recommend that you read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4706102716103727804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1768360452" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1768360452" />
    <a title="Julie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14773738-julie"><img alt="Julie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509454384p2/14773738.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1768360452?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie" href="/user/show/14773738-julie">Julie</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/39350221-the-post-office-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14773738-julie?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14773738-julie?shelf=click-lit">click-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14773738-julie?shelf=austria">austria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1768360452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2645277603972168598">7.5/10<br/><br/>What a strange and powerful -- and ultimately disappointing -- novel this is! I kept thinking how I would have enjoyed it, had it been made into a film. The awkward and unworkable layers would have been stripped clean and one would have been left with a diamond, no longer in the rough.<br/><br/>The "unworkable layers" cannot be ascribed to Zweig alone (or at all?) since this was a work published posthumously, having been on the writer's table for more than a decade, in the 1930s, while he alternatel</span>
  <span id="freeText2645277603972168598" style="display:none">7.5/10<br /><br />What a strange and powerful -- and ultimately disappointing -- novel this is! I kept thinking how I would have enjoyed it, had it been made into a film. The awkward and unworkable layers would have been stripped clean and one would have been left with a diamond, no longer in the rough.<br /><br />The "unworkable layers" cannot be ascribed to Zweig alone (or at all?) since this was a work published posthumously, having been on the writer's table for more than a decade, in the 1930s, while he alternately wrestled with it, and put it from him -- and which was finally published about 40 years after he had shelved it for the last time. Neither did he leave any instructions for this one to be published after his death, as he did with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story" title="Chess Story by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Chess Story</a>. It begs the question: did he want this one going out into the world at all? There must have been some sense of incompleteness or inadequacy that presented itself to him from within this impassioned work.<br /><br />There are many levels herein which pulled at me: the transformative power of war, of society, of poverty. This part trampled my heart for it seems to me he described it accurately and much too poignantly. Coming from a generation whose parents and grandparents were affected directly by the destructive forces of war and its ensuing upheaval attendant with poverty, these passages felt real, and cutting. <br /><br />How does one survive such crushing destruction of home, of self? How does one deal with one's world getting ever and ever smaller, until it all but disappears into a smoky little garret at the end of a street of no account? The cut to the self of losing everything one holds dear is barely noticeable, as each layer gets stripped away; and there is a shock of waking up one morning with only 4 cups to one's name, so not enough china even left over to serve coffee to 5 people at a mother's funeral: this minutiae that Zweig gathers tears out your heart. It is this part of the novel which felt most convincing to me: watching Christine's war and post war existence erode, drop by drop, and distilled into a little room with two beds, with smoke in the rafters from the ill-functioning cooker.<br /><br />Christine has the unexpected opportunity of being sprung from this world for a few weeks when she is invited to visit with her relatives at a hotel in Switzerland. Her life turns on a metaphorical dime and instantly Cinderella is transformed into nothing other than a dancing fool. Made up, tarted up, drunked up, every desire fulfilled and every inhibtion left behind, she takes to the new society like a bird to the air. From the moment she first dons her new dress, one can see the fall. Having been deprived for so long from the liquor of life, it proves too intoxicating to withstand its worst appeal and emotional debauchery.<br /><br />From this point on, the novel begins to fall apart for me because I'm not convinced that such a level headed girl would go so far into destruction. So this is the problem: either give me a girl of questionable character who would succumb to such enticements utterly; or give me a girl with higher morals who would enjoy the moment for what it is -- but don't try to convince me that a Saint Theresa will turn into The Whore of Babylon by donning a slinky gown.<br /><br />Perhaps this is where it fell apart for Zweig as well and why he kept rewriting it for the better part of a decade: <i>things fall apart. the center cannot hold.</i><br /><br />I would have been more convinced had Zweig given me more cause and circumstance to believe in the rage that ensued on the turning of yet another dime. It certainly didn't follow the logic, nor the character of Christine as she had been previously laid out. (It does not follow, unless there was something psychologically wrong with her to begin with -- and this part we are never given.)<br /><br />I found too many problems from this point on, that I lost interest in the story. I rushed to its end and found exactly what I expected: an ambiguous, unsatisfying finish that felt as rushed in its writing, as I felt in its reading.<br /><br />My thoughts here are that Zweig couldn't quite get his character out of the hole into which he had dropped her -- it just feels that he's pulling her off in all directions, and wants it to end, no matter what end.<br /><br />Having said all that, I loved the first half of this book, enough to convince me to seek out Zweig again. His observations on life are disturbing and heart-rending -- and exact. I don't quite understand why this one fell apart for him but I've seen enough to convince me that his other novels might be more rewarding.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2645277603972168598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2028227968" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2028227968" />
    <a title="Versha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7822147-versha"><img alt="Versha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605777129p2/7822147.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2028227968?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Versha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Versha" href="/user/show/7822147-versha">Versha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19210693-the-post-office-girl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7822147-versha?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7822147-versha?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2028227968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15377417424136826864"><i> “Happiness can reach a pitch so great that any further happiness can’t be felt. Pain, despair, humiliation, disgust, and fear are no different.”</i><br/><br/>What a beautifully dark and heart-wrenching tale this was! Like other Stefan Zweig novels that I have read even this had a strange impact on me. I felt restless while reading this. Neither I could continue reading nor could I stop. I loved the way how he forces his readers to get involved with his characters and their story even if they don’t want to wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15377417424136826864" style="display:none"><i> “Happiness can reach a pitch so great that any further happiness can’t be felt. Pain, despair, humiliation, disgust, and fear are no different.”</i><br /><br />What a beautifully dark and heart-wrenching tale this was! Like other Stefan Zweig novels that I have read even this had a strange impact on me. I felt restless while reading this. Neither I could continue reading nor could I stop. I loved the way how he forces his readers to get involved with his characters and their story even if they don’t want to which is evident in his writing he stresses every word, every sentence till the reader gets the hang of it.<br /><br />Christine a simple girl working in a post office in a small village is ignorant of any kind of luxuries that exists in life till she receives a letter from her aunt and uncle to join them in Swiss Alpine resort to give them company for a while. But the moment Christine steps in her new life her old self is dead instantly. It becomes difficult for Christine to be the same old person that she was even after returning from her abrupt vacation and that’s when her life becomes a living hell. She feels caged. Neither she could fit into her current life nor could she get out. She feels a constant embarrassment to lead her life. Her every thought, her every move was heartbreaking to read. But the curiosity of what step Christine takes next kept me glued to this story as it was unpredictable. And then she meets Ferdinand, a war veteran who is as unhappy with his life and the society as Christine is and somehow their coming together disturbs both their lives entirely. Although the ending is abrupt yet I couldn’t have asked for any better ending than that because personally I was not prepared for a closed ending as I didn’t want to know what Ferdinand and Christine ended up doing together.<br />Must say I love this author and I am sure of reading all his works, essays, short stories, novellas and anything that he has ever written. His prose is like poetry to me.<br /><br /><a href="https://my-review-journal.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">My Review Journal</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15377417424136826864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
