<div id="review_162520972" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162520972" />
    <a title="Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2967061-anne"><img alt="Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577653837p2/2967061.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/162520972?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anne" href="/user/show/2967061-anne">Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2967061-anne?shelf=austria">austria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2967061-anne?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2967061-anne?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2967061-anne?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer162520972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10984988642275487167"> The Post Office Girl is a story about a poor, young postal worker, Christine, who gets the chance of a lifetime to have a very brief, but wonderfully transforming vacation from her poverty-stricken life. She is allowed to taste luxury and all that money can buy in a world of wealth and happiness she has never known. This story takes place in Austria after WW1 and is an indictment against Austrian society, or society in general, and the way it allowed the soldiers of WW1 and their families to fl</span>
  <span id="freeText10984988642275487167" style="display:none"> The Post Office Girl is a story about a poor, young postal worker, Christine, who gets the chance of a lifetime to have a very brief, but wonderfully transforming vacation from her poverty-stricken life. She is allowed to taste luxury and all that money can buy in a world of wealth and happiness she has never known. This story takes place in Austria after WW1 and is an indictment against Austrian society, or society in general, and the way it allowed the soldiers of WW1 and their families to flounder in poverty after the war. This book also looks at character and identity and how they can change with changing circumstances. <br /><br />My only quibble with this novel is the introduction, in the second half of the story, of a new character, Ferdinand. He takes up too much space with all his ranting against society which become tedious at times. Worse, he takes the focus off Christine. Nevertheless, this is an extremely well-written page turner, exciting and depressing in turn, with excellent plot and characterization. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10984988642275487167" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_138480308" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/138480308" />
    <a title="Mikki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2757888-mikki"><img alt="Mikki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376784536p2/2757888.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/138480308?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikki" href="/user/show/2757888-mikki">Mikki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2757888-mikki?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2757888-mikki?shelf=austria">austria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer138480308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10905055425446160745"><br/><br/><i>" ...But it might be better not to know you're so poor, so disgustingly poor and wretched." -- Christine </i><br/><br/><br/>This story, which takes place in Austria following World War One, centers around Christine (a lowly post office worker) and the internal psychological warfare she battles over the widened gaps between the social classes and economic equality. At 28, she lives a dreary, poverty-stricken existence dividing her time between work and caring for an ailing mother in their shared one room dwelling. </span>
  <span id="freeText10905055425446160745" style="display:none"><br /><br /><i>" ...But it might be better not to know you're so poor, so disgustingly poor and wretched." -- Christine </i><br /><br /><br />This story, which takes place in Austria following World War One, centers around Christine (a lowly post office worker) and the internal psychological warfare she battles over the widened gaps between the social classes and economic equality. At 28, she lives a dreary, poverty-stricken existence dividing her time between work and caring for an ailing mother in their shared one room dwelling. Resigned to this robotic life, she no longer dreams of what was -- before the war, before the rationing of food, clothing and before the loss of both her father and brother. She has relinquished all hope of regaining the lighthearted youth stripped from her at sixteen and can barely summon up memories of those early years. Defeated and accepting of her fate she lacks all expectation that things will change.<br /><br />Yet change they do in the way of a telegram sent from her estranged Aunt. After fleeing Austria before the war, marrying into wealth and cutting off all ties with family, the Aunt wants to make amends with Christine's mother and in doing so, extends an invitation to vacation at an alpine resort in Switzerland. However, due to poor health, Christine's mother cannot travel and instead sends Christine in her place. She goes and the story then takes on an element of Cinderella.<br /><br />Christine, now completely made over, has slowly shed the mousy, provincial shell of past and has emerged as the "it" girl of the swanky, social crowd. She has it all -- luxurious surroundings and all the trappings that money brings. But all is not what it seems at the hotel and just as in Cinderella, the clock abruptly strikes twelve leaving Christine once again an outcast, emotionally abandoned by her Aunt and left with no alternative but to return to her village. But how can she go back? How is it possible to undo all that she has seen, done, become?<br /><br />Unable and unwilling to squeeze back into her confined past, she seeks refuge in a stranger of similar situation and together they try in vain to keep one another afloat as they rapidly sink lower and lower. Vowing that abject poverty will not be their future, they devise plans of desperate means. Each more costly than the next.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10905055425446160745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1396459997" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1396459997" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4548128-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339423288p2/4548128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1396459997?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/4548128-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4548128-susan?shelf=on-my-e-bookshelves">on-my-e-bookshelves</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1396459997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5467245588733950846">4.5*<br/>There is a saying which tells us that what we've never had, we will never miss, which is probably true, but once having experienced the good life, how can anyone return to a boring and mundane existence without a sense of longing for what has been lost....<br/>Christine, an impoverished post office worker in a small Austrian town, is to learn that for her, this saying is proved to be heartbreakingly true, because having been given a taste of the pleasures money can buy, and then having those thin</span>
  <span id="freeText5467245588733950846" style="display:none">4.5*<br />There is a saying which tells us that what we've never had, we will never miss, which is probably true, but once having experienced the good life, how can anyone return to a boring and mundane existence without a sense of longing for what has been lost....<br />Christine, an impoverished post office worker in a small Austrian town, is to learn that for her, this saying is proved to be heartbreakingly true, because having been given a taste of the pleasures money can buy, and then having those things taken away, her life seems even more bleak and empty.<br /><br />Her days of working in a small post office, where each day is a copy of the previous one have certainly been mundane, and her evenings little better, spent looking after her semi invalid mother<br />The war has been harsh on her family....they have all but starved, and having lost her Father, she now lives in near poverty, existing rather than really living.<br />Her Mother's sister had escaped this lifestyle, and started a new life in America.... When she comes back to Austria for a holiday, she begins to feel guilty about neglecting her family, and invites Christine to share the vacation in a beautiful hotel.<br />Embarrassed by how shabby her niece is, she transforms Christine into a lovely, sociable and popular woman, but when things begin to go wrong, and uncomfortable questions are asked, Christine is dismissed, and sent back to struggle with her former life.<br />Searching for more meaning in her life, Christine embarks on a dubious love affair, and so begins a chain of events which leads her to make some very bad decisions....<br /><br />I was left with the feeling that this book was unfinished....it's abrupt ending left so much unanswered......published after the death of the author, it isn't known if he intended to publish it as it stood, so perhaps it was unfinished, however, great writing, interesting characters and thought provoking themes make this book well worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5467245588733950846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1685687670" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1685687670" />
    <a title="Tsung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung"><img alt="Tsung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298808605p2/4992851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1685687670?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tsung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tsung" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung">Tsung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1685687670" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4880041807656693079">“Keep falsehood and lies far from me; give me neither poverty nor riches, but give me only my daily bread. Otherwise, I may have too much and disown you and say, 'Who is the Lord?' Or I may become poor and steal, and so dishonor the name of my God.” Proverbs 30:8-9 (NIV)<br/><br/>This is a brilliant, thought provoking novel. It is a story of the haves and the have-nots, a story of contrasting fortunes. Yet there is no moral high ground. No one is right or wrong. The rich are supercilious and shallow. The </span>
  <span id="freeText4880041807656693079" style="display:none">“Keep falsehood and lies far from me; give me neither poverty nor riches, but give me only my daily bread. Otherwise, I may have too much and disown you and say, 'Who is the Lord?' Or I may become poor and steal, and so dishonor the name of my God.” Proverbs 30:8-9 (NIV)<br /><br />This is a brilliant, thought provoking novel. It is a story of the haves and the have-nots, a story of contrasting fortunes. Yet there is no moral high ground. No one is right or wrong. The rich are supercilious and shallow. The poor are bitter and envious. All are sinners.<br /> <br />Like in “A Game of Chess and Other Stories”, Zweig’s focus is on the psychology of the characters. He presents them in a detached manner. We observe them from afar, read their minds, analyse and judge them, but without getting involved or feeling sympathy for them. They spiral downwards relentlessly without reaching any “Angle of Repose”. There is no conclusion. Their fates are left open.<br /><br />Set in provincial town in Austria, in a time not long after the First World War, we meet Christine, the post-office girl. Her life is one of toil, drudgery and privations. She has little joy in her mundane, repetitive life. One day, she gets the opportunity of a life-time, her first ever holiday with her wealthy aunt in a posh hotel in Switzerland. For a moment, Christine gets a taste of the high-life and is seduced by it. She transforms from an ugly duckling into a beautiful swan and is intoxicated with the attention that she receives. She also turns from a dutiful daughter (with a sickly mother) to a flighty ingrate. But people are fickle and fate is capricious, and it is all taken away from her, just as quickly as it came.<br /><br />Later, she meets Ferdinand, a handicapped war veteran. He comes across as marginalized and rejected by society. Actually he turns out to be more crippled by his own mindset (in contrast to Franz, Christine’s capable and contented brother-in-law) than by his physical impairment. They are kindred spirits and bond immediately. While their experiences are worlds apart, both share the same bitterness, cynicism, resentment and envy. While there is definite attraction between them, it does not come across as love. Their coming together proves to be detrimental.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />Their plans for either suicide or stealing from post office, is again not presented as right or wrong, but a reflection of how desperate they were to get out of their meaningless existence.<br /><br />The suggestion of suicide is eerie. Was Zweig contemplating and rationalizing it for himself and his wife when they committed suicide while in exile in Brazil?<br /><br />“I still remember the way my high-school teacher used to preach that the only respect in which man is superior to animals is that he can die when he wants to, not just when he has to. Maybe it’s the one freedom you can always count on – the freedom to throw your life away.”<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The novel is nicely written with good pacing and apt expressions.<br /><br />“Something’s happened, something she doesn’t understand. Lightning has struck; now she waits with a beating heart for the thunder which doesn’t come and doesn’t come and yet must come. She doesn’t dare to ask any questions.”<br /><br />The novel is full of contrasts employed to great effect:<br />The rich clientele of the Swiss hotel versus the poor Austrian provincials.<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />Christine’s aunt Clara living in luxury versus Christine’s mother Mary living with illness.<br />The posh hotel of Christine’s holiday versus the seedy hotel of Christine and Ferdinand’s abortive tryst.<br />The frivolous engineer who courted then dumped Christine versus the saturnine Ferdinand who bonded with her.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />A well-deserved five stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4880041807656693079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2028938756" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2028938756" />
    <a title="Kusaimamekirai" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33921897-kusaimamekirai"><img alt="Kusaimamekirai" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591523190p2/33921897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2028938756?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kusaimamekirai" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kusaimamekirai" href="/user/show/33921897-kusaimamekirai">Kusaimamekirai</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33921897-kusaimamekirai?shelf=fiction-germany">fiction-germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2028938756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9717982803308582617">This is my first Stefan Zweig book and it certainly won’t be my last. I’m at a loss as to how I had never heard of him before this short but powerful novel. <br/> Set in 1926 Austria, this is the story of 28 year old Christine, an unassuming and impoverished civil servant barely scraping by and with no prospects on her horizon. It is a world best described as:<br/><br/> <i>“Every morning when I go to work I see people coming out of their front doors, underslept, cheerless, their faces blank, see them going to wor</i></span>
  <span id="freeText9717982803308582617" style="display:none">This is my first Stefan Zweig book and it certainly won’t be my last. I’m at a loss as to how I had never heard of him before this short but powerful novel. <br /> Set in 1926 Austria, this is the story of 28 year old Christine, an unassuming and impoverished civil servant barely scraping by and with no prospects on her horizon. It is a world best described as:<br /><br /> <i>“Every morning when I go to work I see people coming out of their front doors, underslept, cheerless, their faces blank, see them going to work that they haven’t chosen and have no love for and that means nothing to them, and then again in the evening I see them on the streetcars on their way back, their expressions leaden, their feet leaden, all of them exhausted for no good reason, or some reason they don’t understand. They just don’t know it, they don’t believe it or they don’t feel it strongly, not the way I do, this horrible purposelessness.”</i><br /><br /> When she receives a telegram from her wealthy aunt inviting her to spend two weeks with her in Switzerland she is at first hesitant to leave her job and her ill mother behind. Eventually, she decides to go however and this is where things start to go off the rails for her and others in her radius. <br /> Behind some truly magnificent writing, there are some very interesting themes here. What is society’s responsibility to it’s less fortunate? Can the less fortunate once they’ve had a taste of something more than the daily drudgery they’re accustomed to ever go back to that life? Who are in fact the “fortunate”? Perhaps the overarching theme running throughout the entire novel is, who is really ever happy? The rich seem happy but are in fact miserable constantly trying to maintain their fragile wealth and status. The poor on the other hand live only for the sake of living. There is nothing to break up their days, only the promise of another day exactly like the last. As Christine thinks to herself:<br /><br /><i> “What have I been dreaming about during these long empty mornings if not about being free someday from this meaningless grind, this deadly race against time? Relaxing for once, having some unbroken time to myself, not always in shreds, in shards so tiny you could cut your finger on them.”<i><br /><br /> As you can probably guess, this isn’t the most uplifting story you’ll read this year. But what it lacks in joy, it more than makes up for in trying to understand the human condition and what exactly is it than any of us are living for. </i></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="9717982803308582617" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_261637210" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261637210" />
    <a title="Declan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5848260-declan"><img alt="Declan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427664982p2/5848260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/261637210?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Declan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Declan" href="/user/show/5848260-declan">Declan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5848260-declan?shelf=nyrb">nyrb</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer261637210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8782124744583633090"> I liked this book a lot. It has many excellent qualities and it's themes and implications resonate as strongly now as when it was written. We live in a time when people are suddenly elevated to the vapid realms of celebrity because they have appeared on a particular television programme; had a liaison with a President or marry someone wealthy. The newspapers which feel they have delivered fame to these people always follow the trajectory of an arc. The adoration reaches a peak and then, with ru</span>
  <span id="freeText8782124744583633090" style="display:none"> I liked this book a lot. It has many excellent qualities and it's themes and implications resonate as strongly now as when it was written. We live in a time when people are suddenly elevated to the vapid realms of celebrity because they have appeared on a particular television programme; had a liaison with a President or marry someone wealthy. The newspapers which feel they have delivered fame to these people always follow the trajectory of an arc. The adoration reaches a peak and then, with ruthless pleasure, they begin to rip apart the persons reputation. So it is for Christine who becomes enmeshed in a situation she never quite understands. Sadly for her, she never realizes that when something is too good to be true it's because it is! For the people at the hotel she is only of interest because of the identity which they, and through naivety she, have constructed. They want this person to represent a desire or to be an object of intrigue, so they make her as they wish her to be, and because they made her they can unmake her too. The cipher, left floundering when they have had enough, must return to obscurity. Tomorrow at the hotel someone else can become the object of admiration and lust.<br />The second part too has great pertinence for the world as it is now. Soldiers returning from pointless wars; the huge numbers of unemployed or the many with jobs of infinite tedium. They are all present throughout the world now, just as they were when Zweig was writing the novel. Ferdinand may rant on a bit too much but I was really stirred by the eloquence of his descriptions of life in Siberia and the daily humiliations of his working life. It seemed surprising to me that Zweig could manage to be so convincing in his depiction of the thoughts of Ferdinand, especially in his feelings of angst and alienation. <br />Whether the plan worked or not...well, either way I feel that, sadly for Christine and Ferdinand, happiness was never likely to visit them for long. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8782124744583633090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_193088850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/193088850" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3858088-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277161952p2/3858088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/193088850?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/3858088-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer193088850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1368286417606251444">Would it really be a kindness to take a person living their entire life thus far in dull poverty and transport them for 8 days into the very lap of capitalistic luxury, in full knowledge that at the end of the vacation, they would be returned to their previous life?<br/><br/>Christine was one such person, living in post-war Austria with her ailing mother, knowing nothing but poverty and a dreary job in her little town's post office. Her wealthy American aunt, having a sudden attack of conscience, decides </span>
  <span id="freeText1368286417606251444" style="display:none">Would it really be a kindness to take a person living their entire life thus far in dull poverty and transport them for 8 days into the very lap of capitalistic luxury, in full knowledge that at the end of the vacation, they would be returned to their previous life?<br /><br />Christine was one such person, living in post-war Austria with her ailing mother, knowing nothing but poverty and a dreary job in her little town's post office. Her wealthy American aunt, having a sudden attack of conscience, decides to invite her to spend 2 weeks with her in Switzerland. Our mousey unassuming Christine is almost paralyzed with fear when she arrives, believing herself to be unworthy of such luxury, and embarrassed by her old and unfashionable clothes and suitcase. But after an afternoon of shopping with her aunt for new clothes and shoes, and a stylish haircut, Christine emerges like a new butterfly. Unused to being surrounded by beauty, luxury, and elegant society, she flits about and her innocent delight with everyone and everything she sees sets a glow about her, enchanting and alluring. Our shy post office girl relishes her new found confidence and spirited joy. <br /><br />But without warning, her aunt brusquely sends her back home, back to her old job, back to her joyless life. Her mother passes away while she's on her way back. To Christine, life could not be more bleak in comparison to the careless frivolity she enjoyed just a short while ago. She's no longer kind nor generous with her time to her neighbors and those who come to the post office. She wears her bitterness like a thick coat around herself, impenetrable and puzzling to all who had known her. <br /><br />She visits her sister's family in Vienna, and meets Ferdinand, a veteran of the war, and an equally bitter soul. Bitter because he's been discarded by society and the government like an old odd sock since his return from Siberia. The 2 of them form an awkward relationship. It's not one that either finds particularly joyful but it's one they cling to because they understand each other's bitterness. Their increasing anger and disappointments takes them to a turning point in their lives.<br /><br />This is an absorbing work and one that is masterfully crafted. It's also one that lingers in the reader's mind after the last page has been turned.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1368286417606251444" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_944075615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/944075615" />
    <a title="Chuck LoPresti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457963-chuck-lopresti"><img alt="Chuck LoPresti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308763901p2/2457963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/944075615?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chuck LoPresti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chuck LoPresti" href="/user/show/2457963-chuck-lopresti">Chuck LoPresti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer944075615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14657014497976162537">Most of the positive reaction to this book involves the setting, the time, the basic concept of Cinderella at the ball and some mention of the fact that it is "well written". Faced with a wealth of reviews you have the ability to make an informed decision about reading this, if you pay attention closely. What you don't hear about is the fact that Zweig leads the reader through each predictable situation by the nose, elimininating the engagement that might otherwise be present in a book that hold</span>
  <span id="freeText14657014497976162537" style="display:none">Most of the positive reaction to this book involves the setting, the time, the basic concept of Cinderella at the ball and some mention of the fact that it is "well written". Faced with a wealth of reviews you have the ability to make an informed decision about reading this, if you pay attention closely. What you don't hear about is the fact that Zweig leads the reader through each predictable situation by the nose, elimininating the engagement that might otherwise be present in a book that holds its audience in higher regard. No need to attack Zweig here on a personal level (others have done that with feral efficacy)because it's ultimately his book that we are concerned about here. As I read on - I became more infuriated with the simplicity of the language and the trite plot devices. I have read some Zweig that I've enjoyed but you really need to approach this one with caution. If you are looking for an easy read with a plot that most well-rested toddlers will be able to predict - here's your book. Zweig sold lots of books in his day just like McDonalds sells lots of cheeseburgers. If you demand that a writer show some respect for your intelligence - look to Banffy or Moricz for WWI Austro-Hungarian drama. If you want a better US example - read James T. Farrell - simple but engaging where Zweig, in this offering at least, is dull and pandering. I can't stomach another book about women that are only social-climbing, dumb and sexually disfunctional...so uninteresting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14657014497976162537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_261293158" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/261293158" />
    <a title="B. Asma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2338568-b-asma"><img alt="B. Asma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591761945p2/2338568.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/261293158?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="B. Asma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="B. Asma" href="/user/show/2338568-b-asma">B. Asma</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer261293158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18299662377393858573">Author examined the 1920s Austrian conditions and mentality. The characters, Christine and Ferdinand, are at an impasse in their youthful lives. Both (twenty-eight to thirty years old) remember better, hopeful times of earned prosperity and landed security, but that was innocently lost through a force external to them (WWI and its conditions afterward) through no fault of their own and is without recourse to reclaim it. While Christine has meager but steady employment in a village post office th</span>
  <span id="freeText18299662377393858573" style="display:none">Author examined the 1920s Austrian conditions and mentality. The characters, Christine and Ferdinand, are at an impasse in their youthful lives. Both (twenty-eight to thirty years old) remember better, hopeful times of earned prosperity and landed security, but that was innocently lost through a force external to them (WWI and its conditions afterward) through no fault of their own and is without recourse to reclaim it. While Christine has meager but steady employment in a village post office through a connected relative and can expect a pension, Ferdinand is an uncompensated, disabled veteran, while the country's bureaucracy checks identifications and seems to perpetuate itself rather than to be a proper "custodian". The scenes range between shabbiness, opulence, and squalor, between poverty, wealth, and homelessness. Ferdinand thinks rationally, planning step-by-step what he intends to do; Christine takes pride in never being a burden but errs in regarding Ferdinand as superior. Ferdinand sets out a seemingly justified, analyzed plan to give them some freedom. The best of the book is the description of interwar life in Austria and the creation out of those times of desperate characters for whom having prestige and money is the way to at least short-lived happiness, esteem, and love. Is Ferdinand an oxymoron, a morally justified knave?</span>
  <a data-text-id="18299662377393858573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2223797106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2223797106" />
    <a title="Brendan Monroe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22409011-brendan-monroe"><img alt="Brendan Monroe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565543383p2/22409011.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2223797106?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brendan Monroe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brendan Monroe" href="/user/show/22409011-brendan-monroe">Brendan Monroe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=international">international</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=great-character-alert">great-character-alert</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=essential">essential</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=loved">loved</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22409011-brendan-monroe?shelf=austrian">austrian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2223797106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11408023174009585652">A remarkable book by a remarkable man. It's said that Zweig, finding himself stuck after finishing the first part of this book, abandoned it, only to return to it some years later. The second part of the book is, perhaps tellingly then, completely different in tone to the first.<br/><br/>They almost seem like two parts of two completely different books, but they are both brilliant and, put together, the reader undergoes a shock halfway in similar to that that Christine undergoes. <br/><br/>Young Christine Hoflehner</span>
  <span id="freeText11408023174009585652" style="display:none">A remarkable book by a remarkable man. It's said that Zweig, finding himself stuck after finishing the first part of this book, abandoned it, only to return to it some years later. The second part of the book is, perhaps tellingly then, completely different in tone to the first.<br /><br />They almost seem like two parts of two completely different books, but they are both brilliant and, put together, the reader undergoes a shock halfway in similar to that that Christine undergoes. <br /><br />Young Christine Hoflehner is perfectly content with her monotonous job at the Post Office in a village some hours outside of Vienna. She's perfectly content because, having never really traveled, she doesn't know what else exists in the world. That all changes when a telegram from her aunt, who married rich and now lives in America, arrives, inviting Christine to visit her and her husband at a lavish Swiss resort. Christine goes, somewhat reluctantly, and discovers a side of herself she'd never seen before. <br /><br />That's the first part, and I won't give away the details of the second because you ought to learn those on your own. But, reflecting on the first, "happy", part, it does strike me how close things were to ending up completely differently.<br /><br />Another title for "The Post Office Girl" (which is itself only the title of the English edition) could very well be "Ignorance Is Bliss". Christine, it's easy to argue, would ultimately be happier if she'd never known what she was missing. It was her exposure to the rich and luxurious that contributed to her unhappiness and, perhaps, to her ruin.<br /><br />"The Post Office Girl" was published posthumously and even then only published in English in 2008. <br /><br />2008!! <br /><br />That's actually somewhat fitting because even though Zweig's story takes place in post-WWI Austria, "The Post Office Girl" feels remarkably relevant to the 21st century. There are many Christines today, and the rage against the government and at the upper class is at a level not seen in America or Europe in several decades, as more than a few turbulent elections have recently shown.<br /><br />"The Post Office Girl", though, is timeless. It is a book about what it means to feel different than everyone, it's a book about the loss of hope, but, most of all, it's a book about the soullessness and meaninglessness of modern day life. <br /><br />Zweig's books often contain a message. "Impatience of the Heart" was about the danger of that second type of pity, about how acting, or not acting, out of fear of causing harm can often cause even greater harm. And "The Post Office Girl" is about the harm done to the spirit when the mind believes hope to be gone. <br /><br />It's one of the most depressing books I've ever read because I myself felt so close to the two main characters and the situation they find themselves in. But not just me, I think most of my generation these days can relate.<br /><br />This is a book that should be cherished for generations to come, because, for better or worse, it is likely to remain as relevant to future generations as it is to ours and to those who grew up in a shattered Europe after WWI. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11408023174009585652" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14650602713948354295">A very sad and depressing story that I can't say I really enjoyed, however, am giving it 4 stars for the writing as it was a fast read and it did, after all, take place in post WWI Austria during a time of terrible struggles.<p>
  <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[The ending has a couple of shocker's and after I read a bit about how the author and his wife ended their lives, the book seemed to make more sense. Not a book I would want to read again though.<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/><br/> <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</p></span>
  <span id="freeText14650602713948354295" style="display:none">A very sad and depressing story that I can't say I really enjoyed, however, am giving it 4 stars for the writing as it was a fast read and it did, after all, take place in post WWI Austria during a time of terrible struggles.<p>
  <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The ending has a couple of shocker's and after I read a bit about how the author and his wife ended their lives, the book seemed to make more sense.   function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('DD1574odbWo51Vt6emT38QAFbsaCeIwiEtnfy2e2/sKzaBx4XdM5zSg+sHvu7azBM0sbNpdFmHK6rdP+8YLLHA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('O8oTDLsNwGmcwydtMIvuoYNduDJpYINh1wCxwp8iYguEn3abbMOUzo0ozGykArWRsBPNwnxdlzF/dL33CRZX1Q==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_35491454'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35491454-point-cardinal?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Point cardinal<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/7210606.L_onor_de_R_condo?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Léonor de Récondo<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.60 avg rating &mdash; 622 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2017\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer14593932978218420016\">Sur le parking d’un supermarché, dans une petite ville de province, une femme se démaquille méticuleusement, tristement. Enlever sa perruque, sa robe de soie, rouler ses bas sur ses chevilles : ses gestes ressemblent à un arrachement. Bientôt, celle <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14593932978218420016\" style=\"display:none\">Sur le parking d’un supermarché, dans une petite ville de province, une femme se démaquille méticuleusement, tristement. Enlever sa perruque, sa robe de soie, rouler ses bas sur ses chevilles : ses gestes ressemblent à un arrachement. Bientôt, celle qui, à peine une heure auparavant, volait quelques instants de joie et dansait à corps perdu sera devenue méconnaissable. Laurent, en tenue de sport, a remis de l’ordre dans sa voiture et dissimulé dans le coffre la mallette contenant ses habits de fête. Il s’apprête à retrouver femme et enfants pour le dîner. Petit garçon, Laurent passait des heures enfermé dans la penderie de sa mère, détestait l’atmosphère virile et la puanteur des vestiaires après les matchs de foot. Puis il a grandi, a rencontré Solange au lycée, il y a vingt ans déjà. Leur complicité a été immédiate, ils se sont mariés, Thomas et Claire sont nés, ils se sont endettés pour acheter leur maison. Solange prenait les initiatives, Laurent les accueillait avec sérénité. Jusqu’à ce que surviennent d’insupportables douleurs, jusqu’à ce qu’il ne puisse plus réfréner ses envies incontrôlables de toucher de la soie, et que la femme en lui se manifeste impérieusement. De tout cela, il n’a rien dit à Solange. Sa vie va basculer quand, à la faveur de trois jours solitaires, il se travestit pour la première fois chez eux. À son retour, Solange trouve un cheveu blond… Léonor de Récondo va alors suivre ses personnages sur le chemin d’une transformation radicale. Car la découverte de Solange conforte Laurent dans sa certitude : il lui faut laisser exister la femme qu’il a toujours été. Et convaincre son entourage de l’accepter. La détermination de Laurent, le désarroi de Solange, les réactions contrastées des enfants – Claire a treize ans, Thomas seize –, l’incrédulité des collègues de travail : l’écrivain accompagne au plus près de leurs émotions ceux dont la vie est bouleversée.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14593932978218420016\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n Electric, exhilarating, beautifully crafted, Ghana Must Go is a testament to the transformative power of unconditional love, from a debut novelist of extraordinary talent.  Moving with great elegance through time and place, Ghana Must Go charts the Sais’ circuitous journey to one another. In the wake of Kweku’s death, his children gather in Ghana at their enigmatic mother’s new home. The eldest son and his wife; the mysterious, beautiful twins; the baby sister, now a young woman: each carries secrets of his own. It is 1971, a year after the Biafran War, and Nigeria is under military rule—though the politics of the state matter less than those of her home to Enitan Taiwo, an eleven-year-old girl tired of waiting for school to start. Will her mother, who has become deeply religious since the death of Taiwo\'s brother, allow her friendship with the new girl next door, the brash and beautiful Sheri Bakare? Everything Good Will Come charts the fate of these two African girls, one born of privilege and the other, a lower class &quot;half-caste&quot;; one who is prepared to manipulate the traditional system while the other attempts to defy it.\n\nWritten in the voice of Enitan, the novel traces this unusual friendship into their adult lives, against the backdrop of tragedy, family strife, and a war-torn Nigeria. In the end, Everything Good Will Come is Enitan\'s story; one of a fiercely intelligent, strong young woman coming of age in a culture that still insists on feminine submission. Enitan bucks the familial and political systems until she is confronted with the one desire too precious to forfeit in the name of personal freedom: her desire for a child. Everything Good Will Come evokes the sights and smells of Africa while imparting a wise and universal story of love, friendship, prejudice, survival, politics, and the cost of divided       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">About Stefan Zweig</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1190140738p3/25573.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">Stefan Zweig
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,934
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12577975159785698704">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br/><br/>Zweig studied in Austria, France</span>
  <span id="freeText12577975159785698704" style="display:none">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br /><br />Zweig studied in Austria, France, and Germany before settling in Salzburg in 1913. In 1934, driven into exile by the Nazis, he emigrated to England and then, in 1940, to Brazil by way of New York. Finding only growing loneliness and disillusionment in their new surroundings, he and his second wife committed suicide.<br /><br />Zweig's interest in psychology and the teachings of Sigmund Freud led to his most characteristic work, the subtle portrayal of character. Zweig's essays include studies of Honoré de Balzac, Charles Dickens, and Fyodor Dostoyevsky (<i>Drei Meister</i>, 1920; <i>Three Masters</i>) and of Friedrich Hölderlin, Heinrich von Kleist, and Friedrich Nietzsche (<i>Der Kampf mit dem Dämon</i>, 1925; <i>Master Builders</i>). He achieved popularity with <i>Sternstunden der Menschheit</i> (1928; <i>The Tide of Fortune</i>), five historical portraits in miniature. He wrote full-scale, intuitive rather than objective, biographies of the French statesman Joseph Fouché (1929), Mary Stuart (1935), and others. His stories include those in <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i> (1925; <i>Conflicts</i>). He also wrote a psychological novel, <i>Ungeduld des Herzens</i> (1938; <i>Beware of Pity</i>), and translated works of Charles Baudelaire, Paul Verlaine, and Emile Verhaeren.<br /><br />Most recently, his works provided the inspiration for 2014 film <i>The Grand Budapest Hotel</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12577975159785698704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
